<h1 class="firstHeading mw-first-heading" id="firstHeading"><span class="mw-page-title-main">Salt</span></h1>
<div class="vector-body" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="contentSub2"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#searchInput">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Mineral used as food ingredient, composed primarily of sodium chloride</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">This article is about common table salt. For salts in chemistry, see <a href="/wiki/Salt_(chemistry)" title="Salt (chemistry)">Salt (chemistry)</a>. For table salt used in chemistry, see <a href="/wiki/Sodium_chloride" title="Sodium chloride">Sodium chloride</a>. For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Salt_(disambiguation)" title="Salt (disambiguation)">Salt (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:DeadSeaIsrael5.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1728" data-file-width="2304" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/DeadSeaIsrael5.jpg/220px-DeadSeaIsrael5.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/DeadSeaIsrael5.jpg/330px-DeadSeaIsrael5.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/DeadSeaIsrael5.jpg/440px-DeadSeaIsrael5.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:DeadSeaIsrael5.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Salt deposits beside the <a href="/wiki/Dead_Sea" title="Dead Sea">Dead Sea</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Halit_(NaCl)_-_Kopalnia_soli_Wieliczka,_Polska.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2529" data-file-width="3794" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Halit_%28NaCl%29_-_Kopalnia_soli_Wieliczka%2C_Polska.jpg/220px-Halit_%28NaCl%29_-_Kopalnia_soli_Wieliczka%2C_Polska.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Halit_%28NaCl%29_-_Kopalnia_soli_Wieliczka%2C_Polska.jpg/330px-Halit_%28NaCl%29_-_Kopalnia_soli_Wieliczka%2C_Polska.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Halit_%28NaCl%29_-_Kopalnia_soli_Wieliczka%2C_Polska.jpg/440px-Halit_%28NaCl%29_-_Kopalnia_soli_Wieliczka%2C_Polska.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Halit_(NaCl)_-_Kopalnia_soli_Wieliczka,_Polska.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Halite" title="Halite">Halite</a> (rock salt) from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wieliczka_salt_mine" title="Wieliczka salt mine">Wieliczka salt mine</a>, Małopolskie, Poland</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bolivian_rose_salt.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3625" data-file-width="5800" decoding="async" height="138" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Bolivian_rose_salt.jpg/220px-Bolivian_rose_salt.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Bolivian_rose_salt.jpg/330px-Bolivian_rose_salt.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Bolivian_rose_salt.jpg/440px-Bolivian_rose_salt.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bolivian_rose_salt.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Bolivian rose salt from Andes</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Loading_sea_salt_at_evaporation_pond,_Walvis_Bay_(2014).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3264" data-file-width="4928" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/Loading_sea_salt_at_evaporation_pond%2C_Walvis_Bay_%282014%29.jpg/220px-Loading_sea_salt_at_evaporation_pond%2C_Walvis_Bay_%282014%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/Loading_sea_salt_at_evaporation_pond%2C_Walvis_Bay_%282014%29.jpg/330px-Loading_sea_salt_at_evaporation_pond%2C_Walvis_Bay_%282014%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/Loading_sea_salt_at_evaporation_pond%2C_Walvis_Bay_%282014%29.jpg/440px-Loading_sea_salt_at_evaporation_pond%2C_Walvis_Bay_%282014%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Loading_sea_salt_at_evaporation_pond,_Walvis_Bay_(2014).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Loading <a href="/wiki/Sea_salt" title="Sea salt">sea salt</a> at an <a href="/wiki/Salt_evaporation_pond" title="Salt evaporation pond">evaporation pond</a> in <a href="/wiki/Walvis_Bay" title="Walvis Bay">Walvis Bay</a>, <a href="/wiki/Namibia" title="Namibia">Namibia</a>; <a href="/wiki/Halophile" title="Halophile">halophile</a> organisms give it a red colour</div></div></div>
<p><b>Salt</b> is a <a href="/wiki/Mineral" title="Mineral">mineral</a> composed primarily of <a href="/wiki/Sodium_chloride" title="Sodium chloride">sodium chloride</a> (NaCl), a <a href="/wiki/Chemical_compound" title="Chemical compound">chemical compound</a> belonging to the larger class of <a href="/wiki/Salt_(chemistry)" title="Salt (chemistry)">salts</a>; salt in the form of a natural <a href="/wiki/Crystallinity" title="Crystallinity">crystalline</a> mineral is known as rock salt or <a href="/wiki/Halite" title="Halite">halite</a>. Salt is present in vast quantities in <a href="/wiki/Seawater" title="Seawater">seawater</a>. The open ocean has about 35 g (1.2 oz) of solids per liter of sea water, a <a href="/wiki/Salinity" title="Salinity">salinity</a> of 3.5%.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Sodium_in_biology" title="Sodium in biology">Salt is essential for life in general</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Saltiness" title="Saltiness">saltiness</a> is one of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Basic_tastes" title="Basic tastes">basic human tastes</a>. Salt is one of the oldest and most ubiquitous food <a href="/wiki/Seasoning" title="Seasoning">seasonings</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Salting_(food)" title="Salting (food)">salting</a> is an important method of <a href="/wiki/Food_preservation" title="Food preservation">food preservation</a>.
</p><p>Some of the earliest evidence of salt processing dates to around 6,000 BC, when people living in the area of present-day Romania boiled <a href="/wiki/Spring_(hydrology)" title="Spring (hydrology)">spring water</a> to extract salts; a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Salt_in_Chinese_History#Ancient_China_and_the_early_empire" title="Salt in Chinese History">salt-works in China</a> dates to approximately the same period. Salt was also prized by the ancient <a href="/wiki/Hebrews" title="Hebrews">Hebrews</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greece" title="Ancient Greece">Greeks</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Rome" title="Ancient Rome">Romans</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">Byzantines</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Hittites" title="Hittites">Hittites</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egypt" title="Ancient Egypt">Egyptians</a>, and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_India" title="Ancient India">Indians</a>. Salt became an important <a href="/wiki/Commodity" title="Commodity">article of trade</a> and was transported by boat across the <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_Sea" title="Mediterranean Sea">Mediterranean Sea</a>, along specially built <a href="/wiki/Salt_road" title="Salt road">salt roads</a>, and across the <a href="/wiki/Sahara" title="Sahara">Sahara</a> on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Camel_caravan" title="Camel caravan">camel caravans</a>. The scarcity and universal need for salt have led nations to go to war over it and use it to raise <a href="/wiki/Salt_tax" title="Salt tax">tax</a> revenues. Salt is used in religious ceremonies and has other cultural and traditional significance.
</p><p>Salt is processed from <a href="/wiki/Salt_mining" title="Salt mining">salt mines</a>, and by the <a href="/wiki/Evaporation" title="Evaporation">evaporation</a> of seawater (<a href="/wiki/Sea_salt" title="Sea salt">sea salt</a>) and mineral-rich spring water in shallow pools. Its major industrial products are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Caustic_soda" title="Caustic soda">caustic soda</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chlorine" title="Chlorine">chlorine</a>; salt is used in many industrial processes including the manufacture of <a href="/wiki/Polyvinyl_chloride" title="Polyvinyl chloride">polyvinyl chloride</a>, <a href="/wiki/Plastic" title="Plastic">plastics</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paper_pulp" title="Paper pulp">paper pulp</a> and many other products. Of the annual global production of around two hundred million <a href="/wiki/Tonne" title="Tonne">tonnes</a> of salt, about 6% is used for human consumption. Other uses include water conditioning processes, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/De-icing" title="De-icing">de-icing</a> highways, and agricultural use. Edible salt is sold in forms such as sea salt and table salt which usually contains an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anti-caking_agent" title="Anti-caking agent">anti-caking agent</a> and may be <a href="/wiki/Iodised_salt" title="Iodised salt">iodised</a> to prevent <a href="/wiki/Iodine_deficiency" title="Iodine deficiency">iodine deficiency</a>. As well as its use in cooking and at the table, salt is present in many processed foods.
</p><p>Sodium is an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Essential_nutrient" title="Essential nutrient">essential nutrient</a> for human health via its role as an <a href="/wiki/Electrolyte" title="Electrolyte">electrolyte</a> and <a href="/wiki/Osmosis" title="Osmosis">osmotic solute</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USDA2015_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-USDA2015-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IOM2013_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-IOM2013-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CDC_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-CDC-3">[3]</a></sup> Excessive salt consumption may increase the risk of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cardiovascular_diseases" title="Cardiovascular diseases">cardiovascular diseases</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Hypertension" title="Hypertension">hypertension</a>, in children and adults. Such <a href="/wiki/Health_effects_of_salt" title="Health effects of salt">health effects of salt</a> have long been studied. Accordingly, numerous world health associations and experts in developed countries recommend reducing consumption of popular salty foods.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CDC_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-CDC-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-efsa_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-efsa-4">[4]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/World_Health_Organization" title="World Health Organization">World Health Organization</a> recommends that adults consume less than 2,000 mg of sodium, equivalent to 5 grams of salt per day.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup>
</p>
