<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_salt" title="History of salt">History of salt</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Salzproduktion-Halle.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="659" data-file-width="1000" decoding="async" height="145" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Salzproduktion-Halle.jpg/220px-Salzproduktion-Halle.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Salzproduktion-Halle.jpg/330px-Salzproduktion-Halle.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Salzproduktion-Halle.jpg/440px-Salzproduktion-Halle.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Salzproduktion-Halle.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Salt production in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Halle,_Saxony-Anhalt" title="Halle, Saxony-Anhalt">Halle, Saxony-Anhalt</a> (1670)</div></div></div>
<p>All through history, the availability of salt has been pivotal to civilization. What is now thought to have been the first city in Europe is <a href="/wiki/Solnitsata" title="Solnitsata">Solnitsata</a>, in <a href="/wiki/Bulgaria" title="Bulgaria">Bulgaria</a>, which was a salt mine, providing the area now known as the <a href="/wiki/Balkans" title="Balkans">Balkans</a> with salt since 5400 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> Even the name Solnitsata means "salt works".
</p><p>While people have used <a href="/wiki/Canning" title="Canning">canning</a> and artificial <a href="/wiki/Refrigeration" title="Refrigeration">refrigeration</a> to preserve food for the last hundred years or so, salt has been the best-known food preservative, especially for meat, for many thousands of years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarber1999136_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarber1999136-8">[8]</a></sup> A very ancient salt-works operation has been discovered at the Poiana Slatinei archaeological site next to a salt spring in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/V%C3%A2n%C4%83tori-Neam%C5%A3" title="Vânători-Neamţ">Lunca</a>, <a href="/wiki/Neam%C8%9B_County" title="Neamț County">Neamț County</a>, Romania. Evidence indicates that <a href="/wiki/Neolithic" title="Neolithic">Neolithic</a> people of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cucuteni-Trypillian_culture" title="Cucuteni-Trypillian culture">Precucuteni Culture</a> were boiling the salt-laden spring water through the process of <a href="/wiki/Briquetage" title="Briquetage">briquetage</a> to extract the salt as far back as 6050 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWellerDumitroaia2005_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWellerDumitroaia2005-9">[9]</a></sup> The salt extracted from this operation may have had a direct correlation to the rapid growth of this society's population soon after its initial production began.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWellerBrigandNuninger2008225–30_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWellerBrigandNuninger2008225–30-10">[10]</a></sup> The harvest of salt from the surface of <a href="/wiki/Xiechi_Lake" title="Xiechi Lake">Xiechi Lake</a> near <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yuncheng,_Shanxi" title="Yuncheng, Shanxi">Yuncheng</a> in <a href="/wiki/Shanxi" title="Shanxi">Shanxi</a>, China, dates back to at least 6000 BC, making it one of the oldest verifiable saltworks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKurlansky200218–19_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKurlansky200218–19-11">[11]</a></sup>
</p><p>There is more salt in animal tissues, such as meat, blood, and milk, than in plant tissues.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HMSO_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-HMSO-12">[12]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Nomad" title="Nomad">Nomads</a> who subsist on their flocks and herds do not eat salt with their food, but agriculturalists, feeding mainly on cereals and vegetable matter, need to supplement their diet with salt.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EB_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-EB-13">[13]</a></sup> With the spread of civilization, salt became one of the world's main trading commodities. It was of high value to the ancient Hebrews, the Greeks, the Romans, the Byzantines, the Hittites and other peoples of antiquity. In the Middle East, salt was used to ceremonially seal an agreement, and the ancient Hebrews made a "<a href="/wiki/Covenant_of_salt" title="Covenant of salt">covenant of salt</a>" with God and sprinkled salt on their offerings to show their trust in him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template noprint noexcerpt Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wikipedia:NOTRS" title="Wikipedia:NOTRS"><span title="not an objective or academic source (April 2019)">better source needed</span></a></i>]</sup> An ancient practice in time of war was <a href="/wiki/Salting_the_earth" title="Salting the earth">salting the earth</a>: scattering salt around in a defeated city to prevent plant growth. The <a href="/wiki/Bible" title="Bible">Bible</a> tells the story of King <a href="/wiki/Abimelech" title="Abimelech">Abimelech</a> who was ordered by God to do this at <a href="/wiki/Shechem" title="Shechem">Shechem</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> and various texts claim that the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Republic" title="Roman Republic">Roman</a> general <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scipio_Aemilianus_Africanus" title="Scipio Aemilianus Africanus">Scipio Aemilianus Africanus</a> ploughed over and sowed the city of <a href="/wiki/Carthage" title="Carthage">Carthage</a> with salt after it was defeated in the <a href="/wiki/Third_Punic_War" title="Third Punic War">Third Punic War</a> (146 BC).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Salinas_de_Maras,_Peru-20Sept2013.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3000" data-file-width="4000" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Salinas_de_Maras%2C_Peru-20Sept2013.jpg/220px-Salinas_de_Maras%2C_Peru-20Sept2013.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Salinas_de_Maras%2C_Peru-20Sept2013.jpg/330px-Salinas_de_Maras%2C_Peru-20Sept2013.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Salinas_de_Maras%2C_Peru-20Sept2013.jpg/440px-Salinas_de_Maras%2C_Peru-20Sept2013.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Salinas_de_Maras,_Peru-20Sept2013.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Ponds near <a href="/wiki/Maras,_Peru" title="Maras, Peru">Maras, Peru</a>, fed from a mineral spring and used for salt production since <a href="/wiki/Periodization_of_pre-Columbian_Peru" title="Periodization of pre-Columbian Peru">pre-Inca times</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Salt may have been used for <a href="/wiki/Barter" title="Barter">barter</a> in connection with the <a href="/wiki/Obsidian" title="Obsidian">obsidian</a> trade in <a href="/wiki/Anatolia" title="Anatolia">Anatolia</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Neolithic" title="Neolithic">Neolithic Era</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> Salt was included among funeral offerings found in <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egypt" title="Ancient Egypt">ancient Egyptian</a> tombs from the third millennium BC, as were salted birds, and salt fish.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKurlansky200238_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKurlansky200238-18">[18]</a></sup> From about 2800 BC, the Egyptians began exporting salt fish to the <a href="/wiki/Phoenicia" title="Phoenicia">Phoenicians</a> in return for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lebanon_cedar" title="Lebanon cedar">Lebanon cedar</a>, glass, and the dye <a href="/wiki/Tyrian_purple" title="Tyrian purple">Tyrian purple</a>; the Phoenicians traded Egyptian salted fish and salt from <a href="/wiki/North_Africa" title="North Africa">North Africa</a> throughout their Mediterranean trade empire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKurlansky200244_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKurlansky200244-19">[19]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Herodotus" title="Herodotus">Herodotus</a> described salt trading routes across Libya back in the 5th century BC. In the early years of the Roman Empire, roads were built for the transportation of salt from the salt imported at <a href="/wiki/Ostia_(Rome)" title="Ostia (Rome)">Ostia</a> to the capital.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup>
</p><p>In Africa, salt was used as currency south of the Sahara, and slabs of rock salt were used as coins in <a href="/wiki/Ethiopia" title="Ethiopia">Abyssinia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EB_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-EB-13">[13]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Tuareg_people" title="Tuareg people">Tuareg</a> have traditionally maintained routes across the <a href="/wiki/Sahara" title="Sahara">Sahara</a> especially for the transportation of salt by <a href="/wiki/Azalai" title="Azalai">Azalai</a> (salt caravans). The caravans still cross the desert from southern Niger to <a href="/wiki/Bilma" title="Bilma">Bilma</a>, although much of the trade now takes place by truck. Each camel takes two bales of <a href="/wiki/Fodder" title="Fodder">fodder</a> and two of trade goods northwards and returns laden with salt pillars and dates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> In Gabon, before the arrival of Europeans, the coast people carried on a remunerative trade with those of the interior by the medium of sea salt. This was gradually displaced by the salt that Europeans brought in sacks, so that the coast natives lost their previous profits; as of the late 1950s, sea salt was still the currency best appreciated in the interior.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Salzburg" title="Salzburg">Salzburg</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hallstatt" title="Hallstatt">Hallstatt</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Hallein" title="Hallein">Hallein</a> lie within 17 km (11 mi) of each other on the river Salzach in central Austria in an area with extensive salt deposits. <a href="/wiki/Salzach" title="Salzach">Salzach</a> literally means "salt river" and Salzburg "salt castle", both taking their names from the <a href="/wiki/German_language" title="German language">German</a> word <span title="German-language text"><i lang="de">Salz</i></span> meaning salt. Hallstatt was the site of the world's first <a href="/wiki/Salt_mining" title="Salt mining">salt mine</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> The town gave its name to the <a href="/wiki/Hallstatt_culture" title="Hallstatt culture">Hallstatt culture</a> that began mining for salt in the area in about 800 BC. Around 400 BC, the townsfolk, who had previously used <a href="/wiki/Pickaxe" title="Pickaxe">pickaxes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Shovel" title="Shovel">shovels</a>, began <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Open_pan_salt_making" title="Open pan salt making">open pan salt making</a>. During the first millennium BC, Celtic communities grew rich trading salt and <a href="/wiki/Salt-cured_meat" title="Salt-cured meat">salted meat</a> to <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greece" title="Ancient Greece">Ancient Greece</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Rome" title="Ancient Rome">Ancient Rome</a> in exchange for wine and other luxuries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarber1999136_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarber1999136-8">[8]</a></sup>
</p><p>The word <i><a href="/wiki/Salary" title="Salary">salary</a></i> comes from the <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> word for salt. The reason for this is unknown; a persistent modern claim that the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_Legion" title="Roman Legion">Roman Legions</a> were sometimes paid in salt is baseless.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> The word <i>salad</i> literally means "salted", and comes from the ancient Roman practice of salting <a href="/wiki/Leaf_vegetable" title="Leaf vegetable">leaf vegetables</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKurlansky200264_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKurlansky200264-27">[27]</a></sup>
</p><p>Wars have been fought over salt. <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Venice" title="Republic of Venice">Venice</a> fought and won a war with <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Genoa" title="Republic of Genoa">Genoa</a> over the product, and it played an important part in the <a href="/wiki/American_Revolution" title="American Revolution">American Revolution</a>. Cities on overland trade routes grew rich by levying <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Duty_(economics)" title="Duty (economics)">duties</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cowen_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cowen-28">[28]</a></sup> and towns like <a href="/wiki/Liverpool" title="Liverpool">Liverpool</a> flourished on the export of salt extracted from the salt mines of <a href="/wiki/Cheshire" title="Cheshire">Cheshire</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> Various governments have at different times imposed salt taxes on their peoples. The voyages of <a href="/wiki/Christopher_Columbus" title="Christopher Columbus">Christopher Columbus</a> are said to have been financed from salt production in southern Spain, and the oppressive <a href="/wiki/Gabelle" title="Gabelle">salt tax in France</a> was one of the causes of the <a href="/wiki/French_Revolution" title="French Revolution">French Revolution</a>. After being repealed, this tax was reimposed by <a href="/wiki/Napoleon" title="Napoleon">Napoleon</a> when he became emperor to pay for his <a href="/wiki/Napoleonic_Wars" title="Napoleonic Wars">foreign wars</a>, and was not finally abolished until 1946.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cowen_28-1"><a href="#cite_note-Cowen-28">[28]</a></sup> In 1930, <a href="/wiki/Mahatma_Gandhi" title="Mahatma Gandhi">Mahatma Gandhi</a> led a crowd of 100,000 protestors on the "Dandi March" or "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Salt_Satyagraha" title="Salt Satyagraha">Salt Satyagraha</a>", during which they made their own salt from the sea as a demonstration of their opposition to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/History_of_the_British_salt_tax_in_India" title="History of the British salt tax in India">colonial salt tax</a>. This act of <a href="/wiki/Civil_disobedience" title="Civil disobedience">civil disobedience</a> inspired numerous Indians and transformed the <a href="/wiki/Indian_independence_movement" title="Indian independence movement">Indian independence movement</a> from an elitist one with little popular support into a national struggle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDalton199672_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDalton199672-30">[30]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Chemistry">Chemistry</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Single_grain_of_table_salt_(electron_micrograph).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="768" data-file-width="1024" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Single_grain_of_table_salt_%28electron_micrograph%29.jpg/220px-Single_grain_of_table_salt_%28electron_micrograph%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Single_grain_of_table_salt_%28electron_micrograph%29.jpg/330px-Single_grain_of_table_salt_%28electron_micrograph%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Single_grain_of_table_salt_%28electron_micrograph%29.jpg/440px-Single_grain_of_table_salt_%28electron_micrograph%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Single_grain_of_table_salt_(electron_micrograph).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Scanning_electron_microscope" title="Scanning electron microscope">SEM</a> image of a grain of table salt</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Sodium_chloride" title="Sodium chloride">Sodium chloride</a></div>
<p>Salt is mostly <a href="/wiki/Sodium_chloride" title="Sodium chloride">sodium chloride</a> (NaCl). <a href="/wiki/Sea_salt" title="Sea salt">Sea salt</a> and mined salt may contain <a href="/wiki/Trace_element" title="Trace element">trace elements</a>. Mined salt is often refined. Salt crystals are translucent and cubic in shape; they normally appear white but impurities may give them a blue or purple tinge. When dissolved in water sodium chloride separates into Na<sup>+</sup> and Cl<sup>−</sup> ions, and the solubility is 359 grams per litre.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> From cold solutions, salt crystallises as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Water_of_hydration" title="Water of hydration">dihydrate</a> NaCl·2H<sub>2</sub>O. Solutions of sodium chloride have very different properties from those of pure water; the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Freezing_point" title="Freezing point">freezing point</a> is −21.12 °C (−6.02 °F) for 23.31 wt% of salt, and the <a href="/wiki/Boiling_point" title="Boiling point">boiling point</a> of saturated salt solution is around 108.7 °C (227.7 °F).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ullmann_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ullmann-32">[32]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Edible_salt">Edible salt</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_edible_salts" title="List of edible salts">List of edible salts</a> and <a href="/wiki/Salt_substitute" title="Salt substitute">Salt substitute</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Comparison_of_Table_Salt_with_Kitchen_Salt.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3024" data-file-width="4032" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Comparison_of_Table_Salt_with_Kitchen_Salt.png/220px-Comparison_of_Table_Salt_with_Kitchen_Salt.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Comparison_of_Table_Salt_with_Kitchen_Salt.png/330px-Comparison_of_Table_Salt_with_Kitchen_Salt.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Comparison_of_Table_Salt_with_Kitchen_Salt.png/440px-Comparison_of_Table_Salt_with_Kitchen_Salt.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Comparison_of_Table_Salt_with_Kitchen_Salt.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Comparison of table salt with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kitchen_salt" title="Kitchen salt">kitchen salt</a>. Shows a typical salt shaker and salt bowl with salt spread before each on a black background.</div></div></div>
<p>Salt is essential to the health of humans and other animals, and it is one of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Basic_tastes#Basic_tastes" title="Basic tastes">five basic taste sensations</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> Salt is used in many cuisines, and it is often found in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Salt_shaker" title="Salt shaker">salt shakers</a> on diners' eating tables for their personal use on food. Salt is also an ingredient in many manufactured foodstuffs. Table salt is a refined salt containing about 97 to 99 percent <a href="/wiki/Sodium_chloride" title="Sodium chloride">sodium chloride</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> Usually, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anticaking_agents" title="Anticaking agents">anticaking agents</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Sodium_aluminosilicate" title="Sodium aluminosilicate">sodium aluminosilicate</a> or <a href="/wiki/Magnesium_carbonate" title="Magnesium carbonate">magnesium carbonate</a> are added to make it free-flowing. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Iodized_salt" title="Iodized salt">Iodized salt</a>, containing <a href="/wiki/Potassium_iodide" title="Potassium iodide">potassium iodide</a>, is widely available. Some people put a <a href="/wiki/Desiccant" title="Desiccant">desiccant</a>, such as a few grains of uncooked <a href="/wiki/Rice" title="Rice">rice</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> or a <a href="/wiki/Saltine_cracker" title="Saltine cracker">saltine cracker</a>, in their salt shakers to absorb extra moisture and help break up salt clumps that may otherwise form.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fortified_table_salt">Fortified table salt</span></h3>
<p>Some table salt sold for consumption contains additives that address a variety of health concerns, especially in the developing world. The identities and amounts of additives vary from country to country. <a href="/wiki/Iodine" title="Iodine">Iodine</a> is an important micronutrient for humans, and a <a href="/wiki/Iodine_deficiency" title="Iodine deficiency">deficiency</a> of the element can cause lowered production of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thyroxine" title="Thyroxine">thyroxine</a> (<a href="/wiki/Hypothyroidism" title="Hypothyroidism">hypothyroidism</a>) and enlargement of the thyroid gland (<a href="/wiki/Endemic_goitre" title="Endemic goitre">endemic goitre</a>) in adults or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cretinism" title="Cretinism">cretinism</a> in children.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVaidyaChakeraPearce2011_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVaidyaChakeraPearce2011-39">[39]</a></sup> Iodized salt has been used to correct these conditions since 1924<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMarkel1987_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMarkel1987-40">[40]</a></sup> and consists of table salt mixed with a minute amount of <a href="/wiki/Potassium_iodide" title="Potassium iodide">potassium iodide</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sodium_iodide" title="Sodium iodide">sodium iodide</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Sodium_iodate" title="Sodium iodate">sodium iodate</a>. A small amount of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dextrose" title="Dextrose">dextrose</a> may also be added to stabilize the iodine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> Iodine deficiency affects about two billion people around the world and is the leading preventable cause of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mental_retardation" title="Mental retardation">mental retardation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mcneil_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-mcneil-42">[42]</a></sup> Iodized table salt has significantly reduced disorders of iodine deficiency in countries where it is used.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup>
</p><p>The amount of iodine and the specific iodine compound added to salt varies. In the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Food_and_Drug_Administration" title="Food and Drug Administration">Food and Drug Administration</a> (FDA) recommends 150 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Micrograms" title="Micrograms">micrograms</a> of iodine per day for both men and women.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> US iodized salt contains 46–77 ppm (parts per million), whereas in the UK the recommended iodine content of iodized salt is 10–22 ppm.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Sodium_ferrocyanide" title="Sodium ferrocyanide">Sodium ferrocyanide</a>, also known as yellow <a href="/wiki/Prussiate" title="Prussiate">prussiate</a> of soda, is sometimes added to salt as an <a href="/wiki/Anticaking_agent" title="Anticaking agent">anticaking agent</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hansard.millbanksystems.com_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-hansard.millbanksystems.com-46">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEuropean_Commission20013_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEuropean_Commission20013-47">[47]</a></sup> Such anticaking agents have been added since at least 1911 when <a href="/wiki/Magnesium_carbonate" title="Magnesium carbonate">magnesium carbonate</a> was first added to salt to make it flow more freely.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> The safety of sodium ferrocyanide as a food additive was found to be provisionally acceptable by the <a href="/wiki/Committee_on_Toxicity" title="Committee on Toxicity">Committee on Toxicity</a> in 1988.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hansard.millbanksystems.com_46-1"><a href="#cite_note-hansard.millbanksystems.com-46">[46]</a></sup> Other anticaking agents sometimes used include <a href="/wiki/Tricalcium_phosphate" title="Tricalcium phosphate">tricalcium phosphate</a>, <a href="/wiki/Calcium_carbonate" title="Calcium carbonate">calcium</a> or magnesium carbonates, <a href="/wiki/Fatty_acid" title="Fatty acid">fatty acid</a> salts (<a href="/wiki/Acid_salt" title="Acid salt">acid salts</a>), <a href="/wiki/Magnesium_oxide" title="Magnesium oxide">magnesium oxide</a>, <a href="/wiki/Silicon_dioxide" title="Silicon dioxide">silicon dioxide</a>, <a href="/wiki/Calcium_silicate" title="Calcium silicate">calcium silicate</a>, sodium aluminosilicate and <a href="/wiki/Calcium_aluminosilicate" title="Calcium aluminosilicate">calcium aluminosilicate</a>. Both the European Union and the United States Food and Drug Administration permitted the use of <a href="/wiki/Aluminium" title="Aluminium">aluminium</a> in the latter two compounds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup>
</p><p>In "doubly fortified salt", both iodide and iron salts are added. The latter alleviates <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Iron_deficiency_anaemia" title="Iron deficiency anaemia">iron deficiency anaemia</a>, which interferes with the mental development of an estimated 40% of infants in the developing world. A typical iron source is <a href="/wiki/Iron(II)_fumarate" title="Iron(II) fumarate">ferrous fumarate</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWestphalKristenWegenerAmbatiello2010_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWestphalKristenWegenerAmbatiello2010-50">[50]</a></sup> Another additive, especially important for <a href="/wiki/Pregnancy" title="Pregnancy">pregnant</a> women, is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Folic_acid" title="Folic acid">folic acid</a> (vitamin B<sub>9</sub>), which gives the table salt a yellow color. Folic acid helps prevent <a href="/wiki/Neural_tube_defect" title="Neural tube defect">neural tube defects</a> and anaemia, which affect young mothers, especially in developing countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWestphalKristenWegenerAmbatiello2010_50-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWestphalKristenWegenerAmbatiello2010-50">[50]</a></sup>
</p><p>A lack of <a href="/wiki/Fluoride" title="Fluoride">fluoride</a> in the diet is the cause of a greatly increased incidence of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dental_caries" title="Dental caries">dental caries</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESelwitzIsmailPitts2007_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESelwitzIsmailPitts2007-51">[51]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Fluoride" title="Fluoride">Fluoride</a> salts can be added to table salt with the goal of reducing tooth decay, especially in countries that have not benefited from fluoridated toothpastes and fluoridated water. The practice is more common in some European countries where <a href="/wiki/Water_fluoridation" title="Water fluoridation">water fluoridation</a> is not carried out. In <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a>, 35% of the table salt sold contains added <a href="/wiki/Sodium_fluoride" title="Sodium fluoride">sodium fluoride</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWestphalKristenWegenerAmbatiello2010_50-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWestphalKristenWegenerAmbatiello2010-50">[50]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_kinds">Other kinds</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sea_Salt.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="408" data-file-width="486" decoding="async" height="185" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Sea_Salt.jpg/220px-Sea_Salt.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Sea_Salt.jpg/330px-Sea_Salt.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Sea_Salt.jpg/440px-Sea_Salt.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sea_Salt.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Irregular crystals of <a href="/wiki/Sea_salt" title="Sea salt">sea salt</a></div></div></div>
<p>Unrefined <a href="/wiki/Sea_salt" title="Sea salt">sea salt</a> contains small amounts of <a href="/wiki/Magnesium" title="Magnesium">magnesium</a> and <a href="/wiki/Calcium" title="Calcium">calcium</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Halides" title="Halides">halides</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sulfate" title="Sulfate">sulfates</a>, traces of <a href="/wiki/Algae" title="Algae">algal products</a>, salt-resistant bacteria and sediment particles. The calcium and magnesium salts confer a faintly bitter overtone, and they make unrefined sea salt <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hygroscopic" title="Hygroscopic">hygroscopic</a> (i.e., it gradually absorbs moisture from air if stored uncovered). Algal products contribute a mildly "fishy" or "sea-air" odour, the latter from <a href="/wiki/Organobromine_compound#Organobromine_compounds_in_nature" title="Organobromine compound">organobromine compounds</a>. Sediments, the proportion of which varies with the source, give the salt a dull grey appearance. Since taste and aroma compounds are often detectable by humans in minute concentrations, sea salt may have a more complex flavor than pure sodium chloride when sprinkled on top of food. When salt is added during cooking however, these flavors would likely be overwhelmed by those of the food ingredients.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcGee2004642_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcGee2004642-52">[52]</a></sup> The refined salt industry cites scientific studies saying that raw sea and rock salts do not contain enough <a href="/wiki/Iodine" title="Iodine">iodine</a> salts to prevent <a href="/wiki/Iodine_deficiency" title="Iodine deficiency">iodine deficiency diseases</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Himalayan_salt_of_Sa%C3%BAde_flea_market,_S%C3%A3o_Paulo,_Brazil.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3116" data-file-width="3098" decoding="async" height="221" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Himalayan_salt_of_Sa%C3%BAde_flea_market%2C_S%C3%A3o_Paulo%2C_Brazil.jpg/220px-Himalayan_salt_of_Sa%C3%BAde_flea_market%2C_S%C3%A3o_Paulo%2C_Brazil.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Himalayan_salt_of_Sa%C3%BAde_flea_market%2C_S%C3%A3o_Paulo%2C_Brazil.jpg/330px-Himalayan_salt_of_Sa%C3%BAde_flea_market%2C_S%C3%A3o_Paulo%2C_Brazil.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Himalayan_salt_of_Sa%C3%BAde_flea_market%2C_S%C3%A3o_Paulo%2C_Brazil.jpg/440px-Himalayan_salt_of_Sa%C3%BAde_flea_market%2C_S%C3%A3o_Paulo%2C_Brazil.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Himalayan_salt_of_Sa%C3%BAde_flea_market,_S%C3%A3o_Paulo,_Brazil.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Himalayan_salt" title="Himalayan salt">Himalayan salt</a> is <a href="/wiki/Halite" title="Halite">halite</a> with a distinct pink color</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:CH-NB_-_Afghanistan,_Bamiyan,_Bamyan_(Bamian)-_Menschen_-_Annemarie_Schwarzenbach_-_SLA-Schwarzenbach-A-5-20-159.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3154" data-file-width="4265" decoding="async" height="163" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/CH-NB_-_Afghanistan%2C_Bamiyan%2C_Bamyan_%28Bamian%29-_Menschen_-_Annemarie_Schwarzenbach_-_SLA-Schwarzenbach-A-5-20-159.jpg/220px-CH-NB_-_Afghanistan%2C_Bamiyan%2C_Bamyan_%28Bamian%29-_Menschen_-_Annemarie_Schwarzenbach_-_SLA-Schwarzenbach-A-5-20-159.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/CH-NB_-_Afghanistan%2C_Bamiyan%2C_Bamyan_%28Bamian%29-_Menschen_-_Annemarie_Schwarzenbach_-_SLA-Schwarzenbach-A-5-20-159.jpg/330px-CH-NB_-_Afghanistan%2C_Bamiyan%2C_Bamyan_%28Bamian%29-_Menschen_-_Annemarie_Schwarzenbach_-_SLA-Schwarzenbach-A-5-20-159.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/CH-NB_-_Afghanistan%2C_Bamiyan%2C_Bamyan_%28Bamian%29-_Menschen_-_Annemarie_Schwarzenbach_-_SLA-Schwarzenbach-A-5-20-159.jpg/440px-CH-NB_-_Afghanistan%2C_Bamiyan%2C_Bamyan_%28Bamian%29-_Menschen_-_Annemarie_Schwarzenbach_-_SLA-Schwarzenbach-A-5-20-159.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:CH-NB_-_Afghanistan,_Bamiyan,_Bamyan_(Bamian)-_Menschen_-_Annemarie_Schwarzenbach_-_SLA-Schwarzenbach-A-5-20-159.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Two men with stacks of rock salt in <a href="/wiki/Bamyan_Province" title="Bamyan Province">Bamyan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Afghanistan" title="Afghanistan">Afghanistan</a></div></div></div>
<p>Salts have diverse <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Minerality#I–P" title="Minerality">mineralities</a> depending on their source, giving each one a unique flavour. <a href="/wiki/Fleur_de_sel" title="Fleur de sel">Fleur de sel</a>, a natural sea salt from the surface of evaporating brine in salt pans, has a distinctive flavour varying with its source. In traditional <a href="/wiki/Korean_cuisine" title="Korean cuisine">Korean cuisine</a>, so-called "<a href="/wiki/Jugyeom" title="Jugyeom">bamboo salt</a>" is prepared by roasting salt<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELivingston200545_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELivingston200545-54">[54]</a></sup> in a <a href="/wiki/Bamboo" title="Bamboo">bamboo</a> container plugged with mud at both ends. This product absorbs minerals from the bamboo and the mud, and has been claimed to increase the <a href="/wiki/Clastogen" title="Clastogen">anticlastogenic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mutagen" title="Mutagen">antimutagenic</a> properties of <a href="/wiki/Doenjang" title="Doenjang">doenjang</a> (a fermented bean paste).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShahidiShiHo2005575_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShahidiShiHo2005575-55">[55]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Kosher_salt" title="Kosher salt">Kosher or kitchen salt</a> has a larger grain size than table salt and is used in cooking. It can be useful for <a href="/wiki/Brining" title="Brining">brining</a>, bread or <a href="/wiki/Pretzel" title="Pretzel">pretzel</a> making and as a scrubbing agent when combined with oil.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Pickling_salt" title="Pickling salt">Pickling salt</a> is made of ultra-fine grains to speed dissolving to make <a href="/wiki/Brine" title="Brine">brine</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Salt_in_food">Salt in food</span></h3>
<p>Salt is present in most <a href="/wiki/Food" title="Food">foods</a>, but in naturally occurring foodstuffs such as meats, vegetables and fruit, it is present in very small quantities. It is often added to processed foods (such as <a href="/wiki/Canning" title="Canning">canned foods</a> and especially <a href="/wiki/Salting_(food)" title="Salting (food)">salted foods</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pickling" title="Pickling">pickled foods</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Snack_food" title="Snack food">snack foods</a> or other <a href="/wiki/Convenience_food" title="Convenience food">convenience foods</a>), where it functions as both a <a href="/wiki/Preservative" title="Preservative">preservative</a> and a <a href="/wiki/Flavoring" title="Flavoring">flavoring</a>. <a href="/wiki/Dairy_salt" title="Dairy salt">Dairy salt</a> is used in the preparation of butter and cheese products.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> As a flavoring, salt enhances the taste of other foods by suppressing the bitterness of those foods making them more palatable and relatively sweeter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup>
</p><p>Before the advent of electrically powered <a href="/wiki/Refrigeration" title="Refrigeration">refrigeration</a>, salting was one of the main methods of <a href="/wiki/Food_preservation" title="Food preservation">food preservation</a>. Thus, <a href="/wiki/Herring" title="Herring">herring</a> contains 67 mg sodium per 100 g, while <a href="/wiki/Kipper" title="Kipper">kipper</a>, its preserved form, contains 990 mg. Similarly, <a href="/wiki/Pork" title="Pork">pork</a> typically contains 63 mg while <a href="/wiki/Bacon" title="Bacon">bacon</a> contains 1,480 mg, and <a href="/wiki/Potato" title="Potato">potatoes</a> contain 7 mg but <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Potato_crisp" title="Potato crisp">potato crisps</a> 800 mg per 100 g.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HMSO_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-HMSO-12">[12]</a></sup> Salt is also used extensively in <a href="/wiki/Cooking" title="Cooking">cooking</a> as a flavoring, and cooking techniques such as with <a href="/wiki/Salt_crust" title="Salt crust">salt crusts</a> and <a href="/wiki/Brining" title="Brining">brining</a>. The main sources of salt in the Western diet, apart from direct use of sodium chloride, are bread and cereal products, meat products and milk and dairy products.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HMSO_12-2"><a href="#cite_note-HMSO-12">[12]</a></sup>
</p><p>In many East Asian cultures, salt is not traditionally used as a condiment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> In its place, condiments such as <a href="/wiki/Soy_sauce" title="Soy sauce">soy sauce</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fish_sauce" title="Fish sauce">fish sauce</a> and <a href="/wiki/Oyster_sauce" title="Oyster sauce">oyster sauce</a> tend to have a high sodium content and fill a similar role to table salt in western cultures. They are most often used for cooking rather than as table condiments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Biology_of_salt_taste">Biology of salt taste</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Taste_receptor#Salt" title="Taste receptor">Taste receptor § Salt</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Taste#Saltiness" title="Taste">Taste § Saltiness</a></div>
<p>Human salt <a href="/wiki/Taste" title="Taste">taste</a> is detected by sodium <a href="/wiki/Taste_receptor" title="Taste receptor">taste receptors</a> present in taste bud cells on the tongue.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> Human sensory taste testing studies have shown that <a href="/wiki/Proteolysis" title="Proteolysis">proteolyzed</a> forms of <a href="/wiki/Epithelial_sodium_channel" title="Epithelial sodium channel">epithelial sodium channel</a> (ENaC) function as the human salt taste receptor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sodium_consumption_and_health">Sodium consumption and health</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Health_effects_of_salt" title="Health effects of salt">Health effects of salt</a></div>
<p>Table salt is made up of just under 40% sodium by weight, so a 6<span class="nowrap"> </span>g serving (1<span class="nowrap"> </span>teaspoon) contains about 2,400<span class="nowrap"> </span>mg of sodium.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USDA_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-USDA-63">[63]</a></sup> Sodium serves a vital purpose in the human body: via its role as an electrolyte, it helps nerves and muscles to function correctly, and it is one factor involved in the <a href="/wiki/Autoregulation" title="Autoregulation">osmotic regulation</a> of water content in body organs (<a href="/wiki/Fluid_balance" title="Fluid balance">fluid balance</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> Most of the sodium in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Western_diet" title="Western diet">Western diet</a> comes from salt.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CDC_3-2"><a href="#cite_note-CDC-3">[3]</a></sup> The habitual salt intake in many Western countries is about 10 g per day, and it is higher than that in many countries in Eastern Europe and Asia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Strazzullo_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-Strazzullo-65">[65]</a></sup> The high level of sodium in many processed foods has a major impact on the total amount consumed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> In the United States, 75% of the sodium eaten comes from processed and restaurant foods, 11% from cooking and table use and the rest from what is found naturally in foodstuffs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup>
</p><p>Because consuming too much sodium increases risk of <a href="/wiki/Cardiovascular_disease" title="Cardiovascular disease">cardiovascular diseases</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CDC_3-3"><a href="#cite_note-CDC-3">[3]</a></sup> health organizations generally recommend that people reduce their dietary intake of salt.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CDC_3-4"><a href="#cite_note-CDC-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2013_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2013-68">[68]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-He2013_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-He2013-69">[69]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aha_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-aha-70">[70]</a></sup> High sodium intake is associated with a greater risk of <a href="/wiki/Stroke" title="Stroke">stroke</a>, total cardiovascular disease and <a href="/wiki/Kidney_disease" title="Kidney disease">kidney disease</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IOM2013_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-IOM2013-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Strazzullo_65-1"><a href="#cite_note-Strazzullo-65">[65]</a></sup> A reduction in sodium intake by 1,000 mg per day may reduce cardiovascular disease by about 30 percent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USDA2015_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-USDA2015-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CDC_3-5"><a href="#cite_note-CDC-3">[3]</a></sup> In adults and children with no acute illness, a decrease in the intake of sodium from the typical high levels reduces blood pressure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-He2013_69-1"><a href="#cite_note-He2013-69">[69]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Abur2013_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-Abur2013-71">[71]</a></sup> A low sodium diet results in a greater improvement in blood pressure in people with <a href="/wiki/Hypertension" title="Hypertension">hypertension</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/World_Health_Organization" title="World Health Organization">World Health Organization</a> recommends that adults should consume less than 2,000 mg of sodium (which is contained in 5<span class="nowrap"> </span>g of salt) per day.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2013_68-1"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2013-68">[68]</a></sup> Guidelines by the United States recommend that people with hypertension, African Americans, and middle-aged and older adults should limit consumption to no more than 1,500 mg of sodium per day and meet the potassium recommendation of 4,700 mg/day with a healthy diet of fruits and vegetables.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CDC_3-6"><a href="#cite_note-CDC-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Health.gov2010_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-Health.gov2010-74">[74]</a></sup>
</p><p>While reduction of sodium intake to less than 2,300 mg per day is recommended by developed countries,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CDC_3-7"><a href="#cite_note-CDC-3">[3]</a></sup> one review recommended that sodium intake be reduced to at least 1,200 mg (contained in 3<span class="nowrap"> </span>g of salt) per day, as a further reduction in salt intake the greater the fall in systolic blood pressure for all age groups and ethnicities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-He2013_69-2"><a href="#cite_note-He2013-69">[69]</a></sup> Another review indicated that there is inconsistent/insufficient evidence to conclude that reducing sodium intake to lower than 2,300 mg per day is either beneficial or harmful.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup>
</p><p>Evidence shows a more complicated relationship between salt and cardiovascular disease. "Mortality caused by levels of salt the association between sodium consumption and cardiovascular disease or mortality is U-shaped, with increased risk at both high and low sodium intake."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-clinicalkey.com_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-clinicalkey.com-76">[76]</a></sup> The findings showed that increased mortality from excessive salt intake was primarily associated with individuals with hypertension. The levels of increased mortality among those with restricted salt intake appeared to be similar regardless of blood pressure. This evidence shows that while those with hypertension should primarily focus on reducing sodium to recommended levels, all groups should seek to maintain a healthy level of sodium intake of between 4 and 5 grams (equivalent to 10-13 g salt) a day.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-clinicalkey.com_76-1"><a href="#cite_note-clinicalkey.com-76">[76]</a></sup>
</p><p>One of the two most prominent dietary risks for disability in the world are diets high in sodium.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Non-dietary_uses">Non-dietary uses</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Sodium_chloride" title="Sodium chloride">Sodium chloride</a></div>
<p>Only about 6% of the salt manufactured in the world is used in food. Of the remainder, 12% is used in water conditioning processes, 8% goes for de-icing highways and 6% is used in agriculture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ullmann_32-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ullmann-32">[32]</a></sup> Sodium chloride is one of the largest volume inorganic raw materials. Its major chemical products are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Caustic_soda" title="Caustic soda">caustic soda</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chlorine" title="Chlorine">chlorine</a>. These are used in the manufacture of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PVC" title="PVC">PVC</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paper_pulp" title="Paper pulp">paper pulp</a> and many other inorganic and organic compounds. Salt is also used as a <a href="/wiki/Flux_(metallurgy)" title="Flux (metallurgy)">flux</a> in the production of <a href="/wiki/Aluminium" title="Aluminium">aluminium</a>. For this purpose, a layer of melted salt floats on top of the molten metal and removes iron and other metal contaminants. It is also used in the manufacture of <a href="/wiki/Soap" title="Soap">soaps</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Glycerine" title="Glycerine">glycerine</a>, where it is used to <a href="/wiki/Saponification" title="Saponification">saponify</a> fats. As an emulsifier, salt is used in the manufacture of <a href="/wiki/Synthetic_rubber" title="Synthetic rubber">synthetic rubber</a>, and another use is in the firing of <a href="/wiki/Pottery" title="Pottery">pottery</a>, when salt added to the furnace vaporises before condensing onto the surface of the ceramic material, forming a strong <a href="/wiki/Ceramic_glaze" title="Ceramic glaze">glaze</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WASG_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-WASG-78">[78]</a></sup>
</p><p>When drilling through loose materials such as sand or gravel, salt may be added to the <a href="/wiki/Drilling_fluid" title="Drilling fluid">drilling fluid</a> to provide a stable "wall" to prevent the hole collapsing. There are many other processes in which salt is involved. These include its use as a <a href="/wiki/Mordant" title="Mordant">mordant</a> in <a href="/wiki/Textile" title="Textile">textile</a> dying, to regenerate <a href="/wiki/Resin" title="Resin">resins</a> in water softening, for the <a href="/wiki/Tanning_(leather)" title="Tanning (leather)">tanning</a> of hides, the preservation of meat and fish and the <a href="/wiki/Canning" title="Canning">canning</a> of meat and vegetables.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WASG_78-1"><a href="#cite_note-WASG-78">[78]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IHS_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-IHS-79">[79]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKostick2011_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKostick2011-80">[80]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Production">Production</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_salt_production" title="List of countries by salt production">List of countries by salt production</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sea_salt,_evaporation_pond_Walvis_Bay_(2014).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2930" data-file-width="4519" decoding="async" height="143" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Sea_salt%2C_evaporation_pond_Walvis_Bay_%282014%29.jpg/220px-Sea_salt%2C_evaporation_pond_Walvis_Bay_%282014%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Sea_salt%2C_evaporation_pond_Walvis_Bay_%282014%29.jpg/330px-Sea_salt%2C_evaporation_pond_Walvis_Bay_%282014%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Sea_salt%2C_evaporation_pond_Walvis_Bay_%282014%29.jpg/440px-Sea_salt%2C_evaporation_pond_Walvis_Bay_%282014%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sea_salt,_evaporation_pond_Walvis_Bay_(2014).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Sea salt <a href="/wiki/Evaporation_pond" title="Evaporation pond">evaporation pond</a> at <a href="/wiki/Walvis_Bay" title="Walvis Bay">Walvis Bay</a>. <a href="/wiki/Halophile" title="Halophile">Halophile</a> organisms impart a red colour</div></div></div>
<p>Food-grade salt accounts for only a small part of salt production in <a href="/wiki/Developed_country" title="Developed country">industrialized countries</a> (7% in Europe),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> although worldwide, food uses account for 17.5% of total production.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2018, total world production of salt was 300 million <a href="/wiki/Tonne" title="Tonne">tonnes</a>, the top six producers being China (68 million), the United States (42 million), India (29 million), Germany (13 million), Canada (13 million) and Australia (12 million).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup>
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1096954695/mw-parser-output/.tmulti">.mw-parser-output .tmulti .multiimageinner{display:flex;flex-direction:column}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow{display:flex;flex-direction:row;clear:left;flex-wrap:wrap;width:100%;box-sizing:border-box}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{margin:1px;float:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .theader{clear:both;font-weight:bold;text-align:center;align-self:center;background-color:transparent;width:100%}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbcaption{background-color:transparent}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-left{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-right{text-align:right}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-center{text-align:center}@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbinner{width:100%!important;box-sizing:border-box;max-width:none!important;align-items:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow{justify-content:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{float:none!important;max-width:100%!important;box-sizing:border-box;text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle .thumbcaption{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow>.thumbcaption{text-align:center}}</style><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner multiimageinner" style="width:304px;max-width:304px"><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:302px;max-width:302px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bo_Kluea_01.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="1737" data-file-width="2500" decoding="async" height="208" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Bo_Kluea_01.jpg/300px-Bo_Kluea_01.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Bo_Kluea_01.jpg/450px-Bo_Kluea_01.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Bo_Kluea_01.jpg/600px-Bo_Kluea_01.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption"><a href="/wiki/Brine" title="Brine">Brine</a> from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Salt_wells" title="Salt wells">salt wells</a> is boiled to produce salt at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bo_Kluea_District" title="Bo Kluea District">Bo Kluea</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nan_Province" title="Nan Province">Nan Province</a>, Thailand</div></div></div><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:302px;max-width:302px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Piles_of_Salt_Salar_de_Uyuni_Bolivia_Luca_Galuzzi_2006_a.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="1320" data-file-width="2000" decoding="async" height="198" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Piles_of_Salt_Salar_de_Uyuni_Bolivia_Luca_Galuzzi_2006_a.jpg/300px-Piles_of_Salt_Salar_de_Uyuni_Bolivia_Luca_Galuzzi_2006_a.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Piles_of_Salt_Salar_de_Uyuni_Bolivia_Luca_Galuzzi_2006_a.jpg/450px-Piles_of_Salt_Salar_de_Uyuni_Bolivia_Luca_Galuzzi_2006_a.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Piles_of_Salt_Salar_de_Uyuni_Bolivia_Luca_Galuzzi_2006_a.jpg/600px-Piles_of_Salt_Salar_de_Uyuni_Bolivia_Luca_Galuzzi_2006_a.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption">Salt mounds in <a href="/wiki/Salar_de_Uyuni" title="Salar de Uyuni">Salar de Uyuni</a>, Bolivia</div></div></div></div></div>
<p>The manufacture of salt is one of the oldest chemical industries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup> A major source of salt is seawater, which has a <a href="/wiki/Salinity" title="Salinity">salinity</a> of approximately 3.5%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ullmann_32-2"><a href="#cite_note-Ullmann-32">[32]</a></sup> This means that there are about 35 g (1.2 oz) of <a href="/wiki/Sea_salt" title="Sea salt">dissolved salts</a>, predominantly <a href="/wiki/Sodium" title="Sodium">sodium</a> (<span class="chemf nowrap">Na<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">+</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span>) and <a href="/wiki/Chloride" title="Chloride">chloride</a> (<span class="chemf nowrap">Cl<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">−</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span>) <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ions" title="Ions">ions</a>, per kilogram (2.2 lbs) of water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMilleroFeistelWrightMcDougall2008_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMilleroFeistelWrightMcDougall2008-85">[85]</a></sup> The world's oceans are a virtually inexhaustible source of salt, and this abundance of supply means that reserves have not been calculated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IHS_79-1"><a href="#cite_note-IHS-79">[79]</a></sup> The evaporation of seawater is the production method of choice in marine countries with high evaporation and low precipitation rates. <a href="/wiki/Salt_evaporation_pond" title="Salt evaporation pond">Salt evaporation ponds</a> are filled from the ocean and salt crystals can be harvested as the water dries up. Sometimes these ponds have vivid colours, as some species of algae and other micro-organisms thrive in conditions of high salinity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup>
</p><p>Elsewhere, salt is extracted from the vast sedimentary deposits which have been laid down over the millennia from the evaporation of seas and lakes. These sources are either <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Salt_mine" title="Salt mine">mined</a> directly, producing rock salt, or are extracted by pumping water into the deposit. In either case, the salt may be purified by mechanical evaporation of brine. Traditionally, purification was achieved in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Open_pan_salt_making" title="Open pan salt making">shallow open pans</a> that were heated to accelerate evaporation. <a href="/wiki/Vacuum" title="Vacuum">Vacuum</a>-based methods are also employed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKostick2011_80-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKostick2011-80">[80]</a></sup> The raw salt is refined by treatment with chemicals that precipitate most impurities (largely magnesium and calcium salts). Multiple stages of evaporation are then applied.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup> Some salt is produced using the <a href="/wiki/Alberger_process" title="Alberger process">Alberger process</a>, which involves vacuum pan evaporation combined with the seeding of the solution with cubic crystals, and produces a grainy-type flake.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ayoreo_people" title="Ayoreo people">Ayoreo</a>, an indigenous group from the Paraguayan <a href="/wiki/Gran_Chaco_people" title="Gran Chaco people">Chaco</a>, obtain their salt from the ash produced by burning the timber of the Indian salt tree (<i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Maytenus_vitis-idaea&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Maytenus vitis-idaea (page does not exist)">Maytenus vitis-idaea</a></i>) and other trees.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchmeda-Hirschmann1994_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchmeda-Hirschmann1994-89">[89]</a></sup>
</p><p>One of the largest <a href="/wiki/Salt_mining" title="Salt mining">salt mining</a> operations in the world is at the <a href="/wiki/Khewra_Salt_Mine" title="Khewra Salt Mine">Khewra Salt Mine</a> in Pakistan. The mine has nineteen storeys, eleven of which are underground, and 400 km (250 mi) of passages. The salt is dug out by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Room_and_pillar" title="Room and pillar">room and pillar</a> method, where about half the material is left in place to support the upper levels. Extraction of <a href="/wiki/Himalayan_salt" title="Himalayan salt">Himalayan salt</a> is expected to last 350 years at the present rate of extraction of around 385,000 tons per annum.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_Seattle_Times_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-The_Seattle_Times-90">[90]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="In_religion">In religion</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Russian_bread_and_salt.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2216" data-file-width="3184" decoding="async" height="153" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Russian_bread_and_salt.jpg/220px-Russian_bread_and_salt.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Russian_bread_and_salt.jpg/330px-Russian_bread_and_salt.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Russian_bread_and_salt.jpg/440px-Russian_bread_and_salt.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Russian_bread_and_salt.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Bread_and_salt" title="Bread and salt">Bread and salt</a> at a Russian wedding ceremony</div></div></div>
<p>Salt has long held an important place in religion and culture. At the time of <a href="/wiki/Historical_Vedic_religion" title="Historical Vedic religion">Brahmanic</a> sacrifices, in <a href="/wiki/Hittites" title="Hittites">Hittite</a> rituals and during festivals held by <a href="/wiki/Semitic_people" title="Semitic people">Semites</a> and Greeks at the time of the <a href="/wiki/New_moon" title="New moon">new moon</a>, salt was thrown into a fire where it produced crackling noises.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup> The ancient Egyptians, Greeks and Romans invoked their gods with offerings of salt and water and some people think this to be the origin of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Holy_Water" title="Holy Water">Holy Water</a> in the Christian faith.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-92">[92]</a></sup> In <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aztec" title="Aztec">Aztec</a> mythology, <a href="/wiki/Huixtocihuatl" title="Huixtocihuatl">Huixtocihuatl</a> was a fertility goddess who presided over salt and salt water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup>
</p><p>Salt is considered to be a very auspicious substance in <a href="/wiki/Hinduism" title="Hinduism">Hinduism</a> and is used in particular religious ceremonies like house-warmings and weddings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gray_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gray-94">[94]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Jainism" title="Jainism">Jainism</a>, devotees lay an offering of raw rice with a pinch of salt before a deity to signify their devotion and salt is sprinkled on a person's cremated remains before the ashes are buried.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup> Salt is believed to ward off evil spirits in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mahayana_Buddhism" title="Mahayana Buddhism">Mahayana Buddhist</a> tradition, and when returning home from a <a href="/wiki/Funeral" title="Funeral">funeral</a>, a pinch of salt is thrown over the left shoulder as this prevents evil spirits from entering the house.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Shinto" title="Shinto">Shinto</a>, <i>Shio</i><span style="font-weight: normal"> (<span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E5%A1%A9" title="wikt:塩">塩</a>, lit. "salt"</span></span>)</span> is used for <a href="/wiki/Ritual_purification" title="Ritual purification">ritual purification</a> of locations and people (<a href="/wiki/Harae" title="Harae">harae</a>, specifically shubatsu), and small piles of salt are placed in dishes by the entrance of establishments for the twofold purposes of warding off evil and attracting patrons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the <a href="/wiki/Hebrew_Bible" title="Hebrew Bible">Hebrew Bible</a>, there are thirty-five verses which <a href="/wiki/Salt_in_the_Bible" title="Salt in the Bible">mention salt</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BLB_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-BLB-98">[98]</a></sup> One of these mentions <a href="/wiki/Lot%27s_wife" title="Lot's wife">Lot's wife</a>, who was turned into a pillar of salt when she looked back at the cities of <a href="/wiki/Sodom_and_Gomorrah" title="Sodom and Gomorrah">Sodom and Gomorrah</a> (<a href="/wiki/Book_of_Genesis" title="Book of Genesis">Genesis</a> 19:26) as they were destroyed. When the judge <a href="/wiki/Abimelech_(Judges)" title="Abimelech (Judges)">Abimelech</a> destroyed the city of <a href="/wiki/Shechem" title="Shechem">Shechem</a>, he is said to have "<a href="/wiki/Salting_the_earth" title="Salting the earth">sown salt</a> on it," probably as a curse on anyone who would re-inhabit it (Judges 9:45). The <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Job" title="Book of Job">Book of Job</a> contains the first mention of salt as a condiment. "Can that which is unsavoury be eaten without salt? or is there any taste in the white of an egg?" (Job 6:6).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BLB_98-1"><a href="#cite_note-BLB-98">[98]</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/New_Testament" title="New Testament">New Testament</a>, six verses mention salt. In the <a href="/wiki/Sermon_on_the_Mount" title="Sermon on the Mount">Sermon on the Mount</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jesus" title="Jesus">Jesus</a> referred to his followers as the "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Salt_and_Light" title="Salt and Light">salt of the earth</a>". <a href="/wiki/Paul_the_Apostle" title="Paul the Apostle">The apostle Paul</a> also encouraged Christians to "let your conversation be always full of grace, seasoned with salt" (Colossians 4:6).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BLB_98-2"><a href="#cite_note-BLB-98">[98]</a></sup> Salt is mandatory in the rite of the <a href="/wiki/Tridentine_Mass" title="Tridentine Mass">Tridentine Mass</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CE_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-CE-99">[99]</a></sup> Salt is used in the third item (which includes an <a href="/wiki/Exorcism" title="Exorcism">Exorcism</a>) of the Celtic Consecration (<i>cf.</i> <a href="/wiki/Gallican_Rite" title="Gallican Rite">Gallican Rite</a>) that is employed in the consecration of a church. Salt may be added to the water "where it is customary" in the Roman Catholic rite of Holy water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CE_99-1"><a href="#cite_note-CE-99">[99]</a></sup>
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Judaism" title="Judaism">Judaism</a>, it is recommended to have either a salty bread or to add salt to the bread if this bread is unsalted when doing <a href="/wiki/Kiddush" title="Kiddush">Kiddush</a> for <a href="/wiki/Shabbat" title="Shabbat">Shabbat</a>. It is customary to spread some salt over the bread or to dip the bread in a little salt when passing the bread around the table after the Kiddush.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup> To preserve the covenant between their people and God, Jews dip the <a href="/wiki/Sabbath" title="Sabbath">Sabbath</a> bread in salt.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_92-1"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-92">[92]</a></sup>
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Wicca" title="Wicca">Wicca</a>, salt is symbolic of the element Earth. It is also believed to cleanse an area of harmful or negative energies. A dish of salt and a dish of water are almost always present on an <a href="/wiki/Altar" title="Altar">altar</a>, and salt is used in a wide variety of rituals and ceremonies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup>
</p>

<li id="cite_note-Health.gov2010-74"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Health.gov2010_74-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1"><a class="external text" href="https://www.health.gov/dietaryguidelines/dga2010/DietaryGuidelines2010.pdf" rel="nofollow"><i>Dietary Guidelines for Americans</i></a> <span class="cs1-format">(PDF)</span>. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">29 April</span> 2015</span>. <q>African Americans, individuals with hypertension, diabetes, or chronic kidney disease and individuals ages 51 and older, comprise about half of the U.S. population ages 2 and older. While nearly everyone benefits from reducing their sodium intake, the blood pressure of these individuals tends to be even more responsive to the blood pressure-raising effects of sodium than others; therefore, they should reduce their intake to 1,500 mg per day.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Dietary+Guidelines+for+Americans&amp;rft.pages=24&amp;rft.pub=U.S.+Department+of+Agriculture+%26+U.S.+Department+of+Health+and+Human+Services&amp;rft.date=2010&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.health.gov%2Fdietaryguidelines%2Fdga2010%2FDietaryGuidelines2010.pdf&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ASalt"></span></span>
